1. CALL TO ORDER                                                                                       Board President

2. ORAL COMMUNICATIONS
The public may comment on any items within the Board’s jurisdiction, even if the items are not on the agenda only during this portion of
the meeting. However, the law prohibits action by the Board on non-agenda items. Speakers are limited to up to three minutes. If you
wish to speak to a particular item on the current board agenda, your comments will be taken up at the time the Board takes up that item.

Members of the public have two options to offer public comment:
1. Email your full name and the matter you wish to speak about to board@losrios.edu by 3:00pm on the day of the meeting, and you
   will be called on by the Board President during this portion of the meeting.
2. Submit a yellow “Speaker’s Card” to the Clerk of the Board before the meeting is called to order.

A member of the Board may request that an item be removed for further discussion and separate action.

                                                                      Help Us Help You
 Los Rios Community College District strives to make reasonable accommodations in all of its programs, services and activities for all qualified individuals
 with disabilities. Notification (568-3039) 48 hours in advance will enable the District to make arrangements to ensure meeting accessibility. The District
 will swiftly resolve those requests consistent with the ADA and resolving any doubt in favor of accessibility.
                                    Los Rios Community College District Indigenous Land Acknowledgment Statement
In the spirit of community and social justice, we acknowledge the land on which our four colleges reside as the traditional homelands of the Nisenan, Maidu,
and Miwok tribal nations. These sovereign people have been the caretakers of the health of the rivers, the wildlife, the plant life, and the overall eco-social
balance in the greater Sacramento region since time immemorial.

Despite centuries of genocide and occupation, the Nisenan, Maidu, and Miwok continue as vibrant and resilient tribes and bands, both Federally recognized
and unrecognized. Tribal citizens of these nations continue to be an active and important part of our Los Rios college community. We take this opportunity
to acknowledge the land and our responsibility to the original peoples, the present-day Nisenan, Maidu, and Miwok tribal nations.

       A.      Chancellor Search Update: Job Posting and Screening Committee

The Board of Trustees hired PPL, Inc., an executive search firm, to assist in the hiring of its next

close on October 15, 2025. PPL will provide updates on the job posting the screening committee
meetings, and future meetings once applicants are screened.

STATUS
Under the provisions of Education Code section 35143, the governing board is required to set an
annual organizational meeting. In years in which there is not a regular election for governing board
members the organizational meeting “shall be held on any date in December, but no later than
December 20.” The period for the 2025 annual organizational meeting is: December 1-20.

RECOMMENDATION:
It is recommended that the Board of Trustees schedule the annual organizational meeting for
Wednesday, December 17, 2025 at 5:30 p.m.




                                                                                                16
                                                                                                        HOME

                LOS RIOS COMMUNITY COLLEGE DISTRICT
PRESENTED TO BOARD OF TRUSTEES                                               DATE: November 12, 2025

   SUBJECT:                 2025-2028 College Student Equity        ATTACHMENT: Yes
                            Plans Required by Ed Code 78222
                                                                    ENCLOSURE: None
                                                                    STRATEGIC PLAN GOAL(S): 2

   AGENDA ITEM:             Consent Item C                          TYPE OF BOARD CONSIDERATION:

                                                                    CONSENT/ROUTINE                X
   RECOMMENDED BY:
                            Jamey Nye, Deputy Chancellor            FIRST READING

   APPROVED FOR                                                     ACTION
   CONSIDERATION:
                            Brian King, Chancellor                  INFORMATION


BACKGROUND:
The Student Equity and Achievement (SEA) Program was established in Education Code (EC) 78222 with
the intent of supporting Guided Pathways and the system-wide goal of eliminating equity gaps. The SEA
Program is an on-going categorical funding source for colleges. As a condition of receiving funds, per EC
78222, districts are required to provide student matriculation services, such as orientation, counseling
and advising, referral to specialized support services, and other education planning services; establish
on-campus food pantries; and maintain student equity plans that are approved by the district’s Board of
Trustees

The 2025-28 Student Equity Plans (SEP) are due to the California Community College Chancellor’s Office
by November 30, 2025, and require Board of Trustees approval. Each college’s Student Equity Plan has
undergone review through the college’s participatory governance process. Attached are the Student
Equity Plans for American River College, Cosumnes River College, and Folsom Lake College.

STATUS:
The 2025-2028 Student Equity Plans for American River College, Cosumnes River College, and Folsom
Lake College are submitted for Board of Trustees review and approval.

RECOMMENDATION:
It is recommended that the Board of Trustees approve the 2025-2028 Student Equity Plans for American
River College, Consumnes River College and Folsom Lake College.

Plans (Grant: 2025-28 Student Equity Plan)


American River College - Student Equity Plan (2025-28) DRAFT



Guidance
In this section, colleges will be able to preview everything that has been entered in the previous sections. After the review of the
completed sections, while still in the ‘Preview’ section in NOVA, you may download a pdf copy to share with others or you may
click on the “Share” icon at the top of your Student Equity Plan in NOVA to email a pdf and html copy to others who have a NOVA
account.
After the necessary reviews have been completed by all required members in the development of the Student Equity Plan per
regulations and your college’s governance process, please click submit to route to all the individuals listed in the ‘Contact’
session for review and final approval. Once your college’s Student Equity Plan has been adopted by the governing board of the
community college district and all contacts have approved in NOVA, your college’s 2025-28 Student Equity Plan will change from
“Submitted” status to “Certified” status which means your plan is fully certified and completed. THANK YOU!




Details
Assurances
✓ I have read the legislation Education Code 78220 and Education Code 78222 am familiar with the goals, terms, and conditions
of the Student Equity Plan, as well as the requirements of Student Equity & Achievement Legislation.


✓ I have read the legislation Education Code 78221 and acknowledge that Student Equity and Achievement funds are allocated
towards the successful implementation of new or existing strategies to achieve the equity goals established for identified student
groups in this equity plan.


✓ I have read and given special consideration to Education Code 78220 section (b), which states, “Student equity plans shall be
developed with the active involvement of all groups on campus as required by law, including, but not limited to, the academic
senate, academic faculty and staff, student services, and students, and with the involvement of appropriate people from the
community.”

   Campus Involvement & Leveraging Student Voice
   American River College has ensured active involvement across all campus groups in developing its Student Equity Plan,
   consistent with Education Code 78220(b). From the outset, multiple constituencies shaped the plan through shared
   governance and intentional engagement.
   A Summer Work Group of faculty, staff, and managers convened to build draft priorities and worked closely with the Executive
   Leadership Team (ELT) and governance structures, including the Student Success Council, Institutional Effectiveness Council,
   and Operations Council. Deans and AVPs contributed to refining content and aligning administrative and instructional
   leadership with institutional goals.

   Student voice was central throughout. The Student Senate was provided with presentations and hosted listening sessions, as
   did the Academic Senate and the Classified Senate. These sessions created opportunities for students, faculty, classified
   professionals, and managers to raise concerns, share ideas, and help set direction. Researchers and equity professionals
   grounded these conversations in evidence, drawing from the Strategic Plan, the Institutional Equity Plan, and
   Disproportionately Impacted (DI) Reports, each developed through participatory governance.

                                                                                                                            18
                                                                                                                                  HOME
  These processes ensured inclusion of all groups identified in subdivision (b) of Ed Code 78220 and reflected the college’s
  commitment to participatory decision-making. To continue leveraging student voice, ARC will:

            Through ARC’s Institutional Equity Plan, gather data from surveys and focus groups tied to
            student success metrics to capture experiences and adjust interventions.
            Share outcomes transparently with the campus community and involve students in interpreting
            results.
            Partner with student organizations to co-design programs that respond directly to identified
            needs.
  By combining structured governance, evidence-informed analysis, and ongoing student engagement, ARC ensures that equity
  planning is both inclusive and action-oriented, designed not only to comply with state requirements but to advance
  meaningful institutional change.


✓ I have read the legislation Education Code 78220 and understand per Education Code section (c) the Student Equity Plan
“shall be adopted by the governing board of the community college district and submitted to the Chancellor of the California
Community Colleges…”


✓ I acknowledge the importance of needing to be race-conscious in the development of this Student Equity Plan.

  Race-Consciousness
  American River College is approaching the development of its Student Equity Plan with a race-conscious lens, recognizing that
  racial equity gaps are not accidental but the result of long-standing systemic inequities. This framework rejects deficit-based
  thinking and instead focuses on institutional responsibility to remove barriers and create conditions where disproportionately
  impacted students can thrive.
  Disaggregated data is being used to examine outcomes for Black/African American, Latinx, Native American, Asian American,
  Pacific Islander, and multiracial students. These analyses highlight how factors such as course scheduling, placement
  processes, and financial aid access contribute to inequities. The plan avoids a generic, one-size-fits-all model and instead
  applies targeted strategies informed by both historical Disproportionately Impacted (DI) reports and current reflections from
  DI communities. This ensures the plan reflects lived experiences while grounding priorities in evidence.

  Direct student input is central to this approach. Through listening sessions, surveys, and Student Senate presentations,
  students are shaping priorities and helping define actions. Faculty, classified professionals, administrators, and student
  leaders, especially those from racially minoritized communities, are actively engaged in co-creating solutions, making equity
  planning a shared responsibility across the institution.
  The Equity Plan also includes specialized training for administrators, managers, and supervisors on cultural competence,
  cultural humility, equity, and supervision of people from marginalized communities. This complements equity-centered
  professional learning for faculty and staff, such as trauma-informed pedagogy, culturally responsive curriculum, and cultural
  humility practices. Targeted investments include embedded tutoring, mentoring, proactive counseling, and inclusiveidentity-
  affirming spaces such as UNITE and the HomeBases, designed to strengthen belonging and persistence for disproportionately
  impacted students.
  Race-conscious work at ARC is anchored in accountability to measurable outcomes. Progress will be tracked through
  disaggregated success metrics and shared transparently with the campus community. By combining student voice, evidence-
  informed practices, and targeted investments, the college ensures its plan is not only compliant but a vehicle for meaningful
  institutional change.


✓ I have read Education Code 78220 section a(6) and understand it requires the college’s student equity plan to include a
schedule and process of evaluation.

  Local Review Process & Schedule

                                                                                                                          19
                                                                                                                               HOME
American River College ensures ongoing review of its Student Equity Plan through a continuous improvement cycle anchored
in the Institutional Equity Plan. Each semester, the Student Success Council works with college researchers to review
disaggregated Disproportionate Impact (DI) metrics. Findings are reported to the Academic Senate, Classified Senate, and
Student Senate, ensuring broad engagement and accountability. This allows governance bodies to recommend mid-year
adjustments, such as expanding tutoring, increasing proactive counseling, or launching financial aid outreach when data show
new or persistent gaps.
Annually, by June 30, ARC produces an outcomes report aligned with the SEA Annual Report in NOVA. This report is shared
college-wide through governance councils and senates, providing the campus community with a transparent view of progress
toward equity goals. During 2025–26, the college will also update the Institutional Equity Plan and reaffirm SEP targets, linking
this plan with other core planning documents to keep equity at the center of institutional decision-making.

ARC’s current Institutional Equity Plan (2018–2025) has been reaffirmed through the 2025–2026 academic year, ensuring equity
remains the guiding framework for continuous improvement. The plan will be fully reviewed and updated during 2025–26 as
the college transitions to its next long-term equity framework.
Disproportionate Impact (DI) reports are a critical tool in this process. These reports focus on populations such as
Black/African American, Native American, Hispanic/Latinx, LGBTQIA2S+, and Asian American students. They provide
disaggregated analysis of equity gaps in access, course success, retention, and completion, while also documenting and giving
voice to the lived experiences of disproportionately impacted communities. By pairing quantitative evidence with qualitative
student perspectives, DI reports ensure that equity priorities are guided not only by numbers but also by the voices of the
students most affected.
Together, the Student Equity Plan, Institutional Equity Plan, and DI reports create a cycle of accountability and continuous
improvement.

Student Equity Plan Reflection
Reflection

For reference: your most recent SEA Annual Report

Reflecting on the efforts implemented to support your college’s pursuit in achieving the target outcomes developed for the 2022-
25 Student Equity Plan cycle, please answer the following questions:
Key Learnings *
American River College has learned through implementation of the 2022–25 Student Equity Plan that the root causes of equity
gaps are tied to belonging, navigation, and access to supports. Students are more likely to succeed when they encounter early,
connections in inclusive spaces such as HomeBases, UNITE, and learning communities. These environments create welcoming
spaces where students see peer mentors, faculty, and staff who reflect their identities and lived experiences, reinforcing a sense
of belonging and providing immediate guidance. They also serve as connection points where students can access tutoring,
counseling, mentoring.

                                                                                                                          21
                                                                                                                                    HOME
Institutionally, ARC has taken a race-conscious approach, recognizing that equity gaps do not stem from student deficits but from
structural barriers inside and outside the college. Internal barriers include overly complex processes, uneven access to proactive
supports, and instructional practices that are not always culturally responsive. External barriers such as housing insecurity,
transportation challenges, and financial pressures further compound inequities. By acknowledging these realities, the college has
centered its strategies on transforming structures, rather than placing responsibility on students.
Three institutional actions and cultural shifts have had the greatest impact in helping ARC reduce equity gaps and advance
toward its target outcomes:

      1. Scaling peer mentoring and embedded tutoring within transfer-level Math and English, coupled
         with inclusive pedagogy and proactive outreach, has improved course success and persistence.
      2. Inclusive spaces such as HomeBases and UNITE have provided welcoming spaces and visible
         support where students feel represented and connected to resources.
      3. Coordinating onboarding and financial aid outreach—including locating financial aid support in
         the UNITE Center to reduce barriers such as enrollment melt and strengthened retention.
Through these efforts, ARC has fostered a culture of equity that is inclusive, affirms identity, values student voice, and aligns
resources to meet structural challenges. This shift toward intentional representation, safe spaces, and embedded academic and
financial supports reflects the lessons learned during the 2022–25 cycle and is positioning the college to close equity gaps and
achieve its Student Equity Plan goals.


Plan Continuity *
Building on lessons from the 2022–25 Student Equity Plan, American River College will continue working with race-conscious and
equity-centered approach strategies. Three areas of continuity stand out as essential for the 2025–28 cycle.
First, HomeBases and the UNITE Center will continue to serve as hubs where counselors, faculty, peer mentors, financial aid
navigators, and classified professionals provide coordinated, inclusive support. These spaces strengthen belonging, simplify
navigation, and ensure timely access to academic and financial resources. Their impact on persistence and retention makes them
a cornerstone of the new plan.

Second, programs such as the New Faculty Academy, the Line Institute, and ongoing workshops in cultural humility, trauma-
informed pedagogy, and culturally responsive curriculum have shifted instructional and student services practices. Expanding
these opportunities helps sustain a campus culture where equity is understood as institutional work rather than individual effort.
Third, targeted outreach and efforts that connect disproportionately impacted students with learning communities, embedded
tutoring, and basic needs resources have reduced barriers to access and success. Locating financial aid support near identity
based centers has also proven effective in reducing enrollment melt and ensuring students can persist.
Finally, American River College will maintain its commitment to inclusive and identity affirming spaces. The UNITE Center and
HomeBases will remain central hubs where students can find representation, peer mentors, academic guidance, and community.
These spaces have become vital connection points that anchor students’ academic journeys and provide a foundation for new
strategies in the 2025–28 plan.

Together, these discoveries of integrated success teams, professional learning, targeted outreach, and safe spaces offer continuity
between planning cycles. They reflect ARC’s recognition that sustainable progress requires building on what works while
continuing to adapt strategies to meet the evolving needs of disproportionately impacted students.

Goals

Successful Enrollment Equity Goals
There are two related goals for Successful Enrollment: a baseline goal of eliminating disproportional impact (Goal 1);
and second, a goal of fully closing equity gaps (Goal 2). Achieving these incremental goals for the disproportionately
impacted populations above should support the increase of equitable Successful Enrollment for the overall student
population.

Additional Goals
Colleges may have additional goals for specific DI populations and/or the overall student population for Successful
Enrollment as well as goals related to the Vision 2030 Outcome and Benchmark stated below:

Vision 2030 Outcome: Increase with equity the number of students attending a California community college, with
particular emphasis on the number of undeserved Californians. (Vision 2030: A Roadmap for California Community
Colleges: Goal 2, Equity in Access; Outcome 4—Student Participation)
Benchmark: By 2030, increase with equity the number of students attending a California community college by either a)
25% or b) so their enrollments are higher than prior to the pandemic for student populations that experienced enrollment
declines during the pandemic (whichever is greater), with emphasis on reaching underserved populations of Californians.

     Key Strategies for Successful Enrollment - Disproportionately Impacted Student Population(s)
     (500 character max for each strategy)
     Example: Establish and deploy data-driven systematic case management system for successful enrollment for all the identified DI
     student groups, with an initial primary focus on Black or African American students.

     ARC will expand intentional outreach through trained Outreach Specialists who collaborate with UNITE,
     Learning Communities, HomeBases, and feeder high schools. Specialists will provide culturally responsive
 1
     guidance to prospective students and families, with targeted FAFSA/CADAA support for Black/African
     American, Latinx, and Native American students.

     ARC will strengthen onboarding through peer-led welcome days and HomeBase connections, with
     intentional outreach to disproportionately impacted students. Building on models like Ánimo, ARC will host
 2
     cultural orientation events that affirm identity, connect students to mentors, and ensure warm handoffs into
     Learning Communities, Infinity groups, and HomeBases.

     ARC will embed FAFSA/CADAA pop-ups into onboarding, ensuring financial barriers are addressed early.
     Outreach and onboarding will also include career exploration, leveraging Career Center resources to link
 3
     majors with long-term goals. Professional development for staff will build equity-centered practices,
     strengthening support for DI student populations.

Completed Transfer-Level Math and English Equity Goals
There are two related goals for Completed Transfer-Level Math and English: a baseline goal of eliminating
disproportional impact (Goal 1); and second, a goal of fully closing equity gaps (Goal 2). Achieving these incremental
goals for the disproportionately impacted populations above should support the increase of equitable Completion of
Transfer-Level Math and English for the overall student population.

Additional Goals
Colleges may have additional goals for specific DI populations and/or the overall student population for Completed
Transfer-Level Math and English - as well as more specific completion goals for Transfer-Level Math, Transfer-Level
English, and/or ESL Student Completion of Transfer-Level English. Overall, the Completion Transfer-Level Math and
English supports the related Vision 2030 Outcome and Benchmark on Completion stated below:
Vision 2030 Outcome: Increase with equity the number of California community college students who complete a
meaningful educational outcome. (Vision 2030: A Roadmap for California Community Colleges: Goal 1, Equity in Success;
Outcome 1—Completion)
Benchmark: By 2030, increase with equity the number of California community college students completing a certificate,
associate or baccalaureate degree by 30%.

Additional Goals
Are there additional goals your college aims to achieve for any of the identified DI populations and/or the overall student
population for Completion of Transfer-Level Math and English? These additional goals may also be revised or renewed
goals/target outcomes from your previous Student Equity Plan 2022-25. Your college is encouraged to include any
equitable goals for Transfer-Level Math, English, and/or ESL Student Completion of Transfer-Level English that support
the above Vision 2030 Outcome and Benchmark..

       Key Strategies for Transfer-Level Math & English - Disproportionately Impacted Student Population(s)
       (500 character max for each strategy)
       Example: Establish and deploy data-driven systematic case management system and student support outreach—involving
       instructional faculty, counselors, and classified professionals—to improve completion of transfer-level Math and English for all the
       identified DI student groups.

       Embedded Tutoring & Mentoring
       ARC will expand embedded tutoring and mentoring in transfer-level Math and English, recruiting tutors from
   1   disproportionately impacted groups. Class schedules will highlight these supports with clear notes or icons,
       and faculty will be incentivized to integrate tutors. Outreach will ensure students recognize and access these
       culturally affirming resources.

       Faculty Professional Development
       Through the New Faculty Academy and LINE Institute, ARC will strengthen equity-centered pedagogy in Math
   2   and English. Training will focus on trauma-informed practices, culturally relevant teaching, and rejecting
       deficit mindsets. Faculty will be supported to implement inclusive classroom strategies that affirm student
       identity and improve learning outcomes.

       Co-requisites & Community Supports
       Currently our support courses do not have a waitlist making enrolling challenging. ARC will continue to
   3
       simplify enrollment in Math and English courses with support sections, making them visible and seamless in
       their scheduling and normalizing their use as a pathway to success.

Persistence: First Primary Term to Secondary Term


  Data Review/Establishing Equity and Student Populations Goals




                                                                                                                                       30
                                                                                                                               HOME

Transfer Emphasis *
While the work and efforts for all student success metrics are crucial to the success of our students the ‘Transfer’ metric is
of the utmost importance for this 2025-28 Student Equity Plan cycle. As a system, “only 21 percent of community college
students who began college from 2017 to 2019 and intended to transfer did so within four years, and transfer rates were
even lower for students from certain demographic groups and regions of the State”. (California’s Systems of Public Higher
Education: Streamlining the Community College Transfer Process Could Increase Access to Bachelor’s Degree, September
2024, California State Audit, pg1).

The Chancellor’s Office encourages all colleges to examine and address the root causes as to why the majority of transfer-
intending students, including many from disproportionately impacted populations, do not transfer.

Please describe how and why your college strategies listed above for both DI and overall student populations will work to
remove barriers, address student needs, and create clear pathways to improve transfer and meet related goals outlined
in Vision 2030.*

American River College is committed to improving transfer outcomes with a race-conscious focus on Black and African
American, Latinx, and Native American students and their intersectionalities. The college will strategically align SEAP
resources with grants to strengthen transfer strategies that remove barriers, meet student needs, and create clear,
identity-affirming pathways consistent with Vision 2030.
A major barrier is belonging. Many students report not seeing themselves represented in transfer services. ARC will embed
transfer counseling and mentoring within UNITE and HomeBases, where students experience culturally affirming spaces
and connect with counselors, faculty, and mentors who reflect their lived experiences. Strong Workforce and Perkins
partnerships will link students with career role models, reinforcing transfer expectations and future opportunities.
Financial barriers remain critical. Through Beaver Cares, ARC will provide textbooks, laptop loans, food distribution, and
housing assistance. Wraparound supports such as the Child Development Center, CalWORKs, and EOPS will be integrated
into onboarding and counseling so students’ basic needs are addressed alongside academic planning. Resource
information will also be embedded into Canvas to normalize and simplify access.

Course access and clarity are essential to transfer momentum. ARC will refine enrollment management to ensure students
can access required courses on time. Counselors, articulation officers, and HomeBase liaisons will expand communication
about ADTs, the Common Course Numbering system, and transfer pathways. Instructional departments will align
curriculum and counseling to clarify how courses map to majors, careers, and transfer institutions.
Targeted academic supports will expand. Embedded tutoring, peer mentoring, and proactive counseling will be paired
with faculty development in culturally responsive and trauma-informed pedagogy. Community partnerships will connect
students to internships, scholarships, and leadership opportunities that build skills and confidence for four-year success.
Together, these strategies—identity-affirming counseling, basic needs integration, refined course access, and expanded
academic supports—directly address barriers and create clear pathways to transfer. By centering the experiences of Black



                                                                                                                             39
                                                                                                                                         HOME
  and African American, Latinx, and Native American students, ARC ensures transfer is attainable and aligned with the
  equity goals of Vision 2030.
  *Vision 2030: A Roadmap for California Community Colleges: Goal 1, Equity in Success; Outcomes 2d and 2e—Baccalaureate Attainment
  includes the following: (I) Increase with equity the number of California community college students who transfer to CSU or UC. (II)
  Increase with equity the number of California community college students who transfer to non-profit private/independent four-year
  institutions.




Intensive Focus on Population(s) Experiencing Disproportionate Impact (DI)
Student Population(s) Experiencing DI for Intensive Focus


  DI Student Population
  Black or African American


  Current Challenges/Barriers *
  Consider your institution policies, processes, practices, and culture: what current structures are challenges/barriers for
  the identified student population experiencing DI at your college?

  American River College recognizes that Black and African American students face persistent structural barriers that limit
  equitable access and success. These barriers are shaped by institutional policies, practices, and culture as well as external
  factors that intersect with students’ academic journeys.

  For Black and African American students, belonging gaps remain significant. Many report feeling isolated within a large
  institution that can feel impersonal and difficult to navigate. Without clear touchpoints, students may not know where to
  seek help or how to connect with the right services. This lack of belonging compounds other challenges, including
  navigating financial aid, enrollment, and business processes that are often complex and time consuming. These processes
  frequently result in delays, missed opportunities, or unnecessary stress that undermines persistence.
  Limited access to high-demand courses also creates inequities, particularly when course bottlenecks delay timely transfer
  or completion. Students balancing work and family obligations face even greater challenges when schedules are inflexible
  or when they must repeat attempts to enroll in required classes.
  Black and African American students also encounter experiences of bias and microaggressions, which can diminish trust
  and motivation. The absence of visible faculty, staff, and peer role models further reinforces feelings of exclusion. To
  counter this, intentional strategies are needed that provide identity-affirming spaces, mentorship, and visible
  representation. Efforts must also include proactive approaches to de-escalation, empathy, and culturally competent
  support to reduce the negative impacts of bias and create conditions for success.

  Together, these barriers underscore the need for ARC to expand identity-affirming supports in HomeBases and UNITE,
  simplify institutional processes, increase access to required courses, and ensure that Black and African American students
  encounter a campus culture where they are seen, valued, and supported in their educational goals.


  Action Plan for Ideal Institution *
  What is your college’s action plan to achieve your identified goals across all five metrics for this specific student
  population? Please include, at minimum, the following information in the action plan:

     1. How will your college address and overcome the challenges and/or barriers shared above?
     2. What specific strategies will be implemented, especially across academic and student affairs, and what will


                                                                                                                                40
                                                                                                                                   HOME
    success look like?
    3. What resources, structures, and/or support will be utilized to effectively accomplish this action plan?

ARC will foster belonging for Black and African American students by expanding inclusive and culturally affirming spaces
within UNITE and HomeBases, staffed by counselors, faculty, and peer mentors who reflect students’ identities and lived
experiences. Proactive counseling and case management will connect students to tutoring, financial aid, and mental
health resources before barriers escalate into crises.
Managers, faculty, and staff will be cross-trained in cultural humility, trauma-informed practices, and de-escalation to
reduce systemic barriers and improve the quality of service interactions. Financial literacy workshops will provide
students with tools to manage aid, navigate enrollment and business processes, and plan long-term for transfer and
career goals. Sessions on the ethical and effective use of artificial intelligence will help students engage confidently with
emerging technologies.
Community partnerships will expand access to employment, housing, food security, and wraparound supports that
reinforce academic persistence. By leveraging relationships with workforce organizations and local employers, ARC will
connect students with internships and career opportunities aligned to their majors.
Success will be measured through higher persistence rates, improved financial aid access and utilization, and increased
degree completion and transfer outcomes for Black and African American students.



DI Student Population
Hispanic


Current Challenges/Barriers *

Consider your institution policies, processes, practices, and culture: what current structures are challenges/barriers for
the identified student population experiencing DI at your college?

American River College recognizes that Latinx students face structural barriers that limit equitable access and persistence.
These barriers are shaped by institutional processes as well as external cultural, linguistic, and financial realities that
intersect with students’ academic journeys.
For many Latinx students, financial aid remains a major obstacle. The SAP appeal process is burdensome, requiring
documentation and time commitments that create unnecessary stress. Complex enrollment paperwork adds to the
challenge, often requiring translation or additional guidance. For families with limited English proficiency and being a
first-generation college student, these systems can feel overwhelming and discouraging.

Latinx students also face systemic barriers related to awareness and information. Proactive and culturally-responsive
counseling can connect students to resources, support and knowledge of educational goals, for many lack clear guidance
on transfer pathways, certificate programs, and career-aligned options. Without this knowledge, they may make
enrollment decisions that do not align with their long-term goals, delaying or preventing timely completion. Those who
are undocumented and of mixed-status families further complicate planning, introducing fear, uncertainty, and
hesitation in accessing available resources.
The large size of the campus can leave Latinx students feeling disconnected. Without intentional outreach and culturally
affirming spaces like El Centro, students may not find community or know where to build meaningful connections.
Balancing family, work, and school responsibilities further strains persistence, particularly when resources are siloed or
difficult to access.
ARC must therefore ensure that Latinx students encounter financial aid processes that are clear and equitable, outreach
that is linguistically and culturally responsive, and support networks that actively connect students to career and transfer
opportunities. By strengthening HomeBases, the UNITE Center, and Learning Communities, and embedding bilingual
resources, ARC can create a climate where Latinx students thrive and persist through to completion and transfer.


                                                                                                                              41
                                                                                                                                   HOME
Action Plan for Ideal Institution *
What is your college’s action plan to achieve your identified goals across all five metrics for this specific student
population? Please include, at minimum, the following information in the action plan:

   1. How will your college address and overcome the challenges and/or barriers shared above?
   2. What specific strategies will be implemented, especially across academic and student affairs, and what will
   success look like?
   3. What resources, structures, and/or support will be utilized to effectively accomplish this action plan?

ARC will expand bilingual counseling, workshops, and family engagement opportunities to help Latinx students and their
families navigate enrollment, financial aid, and transfer. Proactive counseling and case management will connect
students to tutoring, financial aid, and mental health resources before barriers escalate into crises. SAP appeal advocacy
will ensure students remain eligible for financial aid, with targeted support plans to keep them on the path to completion.

Embedded tutoring in ESL, English, and gateway math courses will strengthen academic persistence and ensure early
success in transfer-level coursework. HomeBases and the UNITE Center will serve as cultural anchors, providing
mentoring, proactive counseling, and guided pathway support that are inclusive and affirm identity and strengthen
belonging.
Partnerships with Latinx-serving community organizations will expand access to childcare, housing, food assistance, and
scholarships. These partnerships will reinforce the academic supports already available on campus, creating a
comprehensive safety net that addresses both academic and non-academic needs.
Success will be measured by improved FAFSA/CADAA completion rates, higher persistence in English and math sequences,
and increased rates of degree attainment and transfer among Latinx students.



DI Student Population
American Indian/Alaska Native


Current Challenges/Barriers *
Consider your institution policies, processes, practices, and culture: what current structures are challenges/barriers for
the identified student population experiencing DI at your college?

American River College recognizes that Native American students face persistent structural barriers that limit equitable
access and success. These barriers are shaped by historic and ongoing inequities, institutional practices, and cultural
erasure across higher education.
For Native American students, belonging gaps are intensified by the size of the institution and the absence of visibility in
curriculum, student services, and campus life. Many students do not see themselves reflected in faculty, staff, or
programming, which contributes to feelings of isolation and distrust of the institution. Building trust is essential, given the
long history of exclusion and inequity in education.

Financial aid processes, particularly FAFSA and CADAA completion, are often confusing and lack culturally relevant
support. Communications about deadlines and requirements are not always clear, and securing counseling appointments
can be difficult. Geographic distance and digital access barriers also limit participation, particularly for students from rural
tribal communities.
Native American may experience barriers related to course planning and access. They could benefit from proactive
guidance, avoiding delays in course sequencing can prevent timely transfer or completion. The absence of Native
representation in advising and counseling roles compounds the challenge, leaving students without role models or
mentors who understand their unique cultural and academic needs.
These realities underscore the importance of embedding transfer and academic supports within the Native American
Resource Center, ensuring students have identity-affirming spaces and access to culturally competent staff. ARC must also

                                                                                                                          42
                                                                                                                                  HOME
   strengthen partnerships with tribal organizations and expand awareness of scholarships, internships, and transfer
   opportunities specific to Native communities.
   By increasing visibility, building trust, and embedding culturally relevant supports across financial aid, counseling, and
   course planning, ARC can ensure Native American students experience an environment where persistence and transfer
   are attainable and aligned with their identities and aspirations.


   Action Plan for Ideal Institution *

   What is your college’s action plan to achieve your identified goals across all five metrics for this specific student
   population? Please include, at minimum, the following information in the action plan:

       1. How will your college address and overcome the challenges and/or barriers shared above?
       2. What specific strategies will be implemented, especially across academic and student affairs, and what will
       success look like?
       3. What resources, structures, and/or support will be utilized to effectively accomplish this action plan?

   ARC will strengthen authentic, reciprocal, and sustained partnerships with Tribal Nations and Native community
   organizations to co-create culturally responsive supports that reflect the sovereignty, traditions, and lived experiences of
   Native American students. This work begins with acknowledging that ARC exists on unceded tribal homelands and that
   land expropriation continues to shape inequities today.
   Resources, Structures, and SupportARC will prioritize the educational success and wellness of AI/AN students by
   supporting the Native American Resource Center and Native American Student Support and Success Program and
   creating dedicated spaces within HomeBases that foster community, visibility, and cultural programming. A unique Native
   student advising and financial aid support system—developed in consultation with local tribal partners—will connect
   students to tutoring, enrollment, and financial resources. Proactive counseling and case management will be embedded
   to ensure students receive timely support before barriers escalate into crises.
   Curriculum, Faculty, and Staff DevelopmentFaculty and staff will receive professional learning in cultural humility and the
   historical context of Native education, including ongoing Applied Allies training for employees. ARC will expand
   recruitment strategies to increase representation of Native faculty and staff and integrate Native history, knowledge
   systems, and contemporary issues into curriculum.

   Access and EquityTechnology access will be expanded through laptops, Wi-Fi hotspots, and digital literacy workshops to
   reduce geographic and digital inequities. ARC will also partner with local Tribal Nations to explore rematriated campus
   spaces for cultural practices, contemplative gardens, and educational plant walks, co-managed with tribal partners.
   Accountability and Measurement
   Success will be measured through stronger FAFSA/CADAA completion, improved persistence, higher course success rates,
   and increased transfer outcomes for Native American students. These outcomes will be supported by community-
   building events, targeted scholarships, and continuous collaboration with the Native Nations Advisory Committee.

Definitions:




                                                                                                                           43
                                                                                                                                     HOME
Cohort: New, First-Time, Non-Special Admit Unduplicated Students for that Term
Exempt Students: To the extent possible, please do not include students who are exempt from student education plans in your count
of students who have received a comprehensive student education plan. Refer to Title 5 Section 55532 for a list of possible exempt
students.
Comprehensive Student Education Plans: A comprehensive education plan is at least 2 terms in length and should reflect the
number of terms required to achieve the student’s declared course of study. (Current MIS Data Element Dictionary SS09 for
Student Credit Education Plan).

Note: The following is a newly proposed 2025 MIS definition for comprehensive education plans: A comprehensive education
plan is at least 2 terms in length and should, at minimum, comply with Title 55524 Student Education Plans and include the student’s
declared course of study along with all required courses and other requirements needed to complete each term to achieve the
student’s declared course of study (i.e. degree, certificate, transfer, apprenticeship).
