                                                                                                   141
                                                    HOME



immediately and directly receive resources and
support.

This includes a recognition that many of the
African-American Students enrolled at FLC may be
in other sites/modalities than in traditional
on-ground courses at the FLC Main Campus; they
may be enrolled Online Asynchronous Programs,
at the Rancho Cordova Center, in the PREP
Program, etc. While the logical place to launch
and house the Umoja Program is in FLC’s Equity
Center, steps need to be taken in order to ensure
that the current, new and future populations of
African American students are effectively engaged
and recruited to the program at multiple sites,
and through multiple modalities. Transportation
barriers (as listed above) have been
repeatedly identified as preventing access to
the main campus and must be addressed, in
order to enable more African American
students to access the Umoja Program, and
other Equity Center services housed at the
main campus. The eventual goal is to expand
offerings designed to assist African-American
students at the Rancho Cordova Center. In
the more immediate future, expanding
transportation between the FLC Main
Campus and the Rancho Cordova Center can
serve to ameliorate the lack of special
services for Intensive Focus populations at
RCC.

An additional best practice of training faculty
and staff through a comprehensive Equity
Training was recommended by the CCCCO in
their seminar on SEAP planning. Reinstituting
this at FLC is a key strategy to address the
persistent gaps in academic achievement for
African-American students. Integrating this
into FLC’s New Faculty Academy will ensure
that all new full-time faculty will receive this
training, but it also needs to be offered and
incentivized for adjunct faculty who interact
with the majority of FLC students. This can be
achieved through making the Equity Training




                                             142
                                                      HOME



eligible for college service hours similar to
curricular development/committee service,
etc. and by making the training eligible for
salary schedule advancement.These trainings
are not equity compliance (hiring committee)
nor data inquiry (DIG for Equity) trainings, but
comprehensive equity trainings such as those
which The National Equity Project
(https://www.nationalequityproject.org/) and
USC’s Center for Urban Education
(https://www.cue-tools.usc.edu/) regularly
enlisted to provide for FLC and Los Rios prior
to the COVID-19 Global Pandemic. Resuming
these trainings annually and mandating them
for new faculty aligns with best practices as
presented by the CCCCO.

FLC is making important strides toward a more
inclusive and equitable college environment.
However, lasting equity requires coordinated
action, institutional commitment, and sustained
investment. Through the launch of Umoja,
expansion of DI student services, strengthened
online and center-based equity efforts, and
deeper faculty/staff training, FLC is positioned to
become a leader in equitable student success.

Hispanic / Latino: Challenges / Barriers   Latina/o/x students at Folsom Lake College
(2500 characters max)                      continue to face a range of persistent systemic
                                           and structural barriers that impact access,
                                           success, and completion. These challenges reflect
                                           broader society inequities as well as gaps in
                                           institutional support. Many struggle with basic
                                           needs, including housing instability, unreliable
                                           transportation, food insecurity, and limited access
                                           to affordable childcare. These barriers directly
                                           affect attendance, focus, and persistence, making
                                           it difficult for students to maintain academic
                                           progress. In addition, Latina/o/x students from
                                           mixed status families face additional barriers to
                                           education, and their safety is threatened by
                                           current government actions.

                                           Limited access to academic and career support
                                           creates obstacles. Large numbers of
                                           Hispanic/Latino Folsom Lake College students use
                                           existing Tutoring and academic mentoring
                                           programs and typically experience much higher
                                           success and persistence levels than their peers
                                           who don’t use these programs; the impact could
                                           be expanded by increasing availability, and
                                           culturally relevant delivery methods. Many
                                           students lack access to internships, research
                                           opportunities, and career exploration programs;
                                           resources that are vital to long-term academic
                                           and professional success. These gaps contribute
                                           to low visibility in high-impact programs and
                                           fewer pathways to transfer or employment.

                                           Transfer remains a significant hurdle for many
                                           Latina/o/x students, who may be first-generation
                                           college-goers navigating complex systems without
                                           clear guidance or consistent advising. Language
                                           barriers, limited representation among faculty
                                           and staff, and unfamiliarity with transfer
                                           processes can all contribute to discouragement or
                                           delays.

                                           Cultural invisibility further impacts students'
                                           sense of belonging. A lack of support for cultural
                                           events, clubs, and heritage celebrations makes it
                                           harder for Latina/o/x students to see their
                                           identities and contributions reflected on campus.
                                           This absence can reinforce feelings of isolation
                                           and disengagement.



                                                                                          144
                                                       HOME




Outside the classroom, changing federal
immigration policies, racial profiling, and the fear
of ICE raids generate anxiety and instability for
many students and families. Even those with legal
status or citizenship may carry the burden of fear
due to prevailing stereotypes and anti-immigrant
rhetoric and actions. These experiences affect
students’ mental health, campus engagement,
and willingness to seek support.

To create an inclusive and supportive
environment for Latina/o/x students, FLC must
continue to address these intersecting challenges
with intentionality, cultural responsiveness, and
sustained institutional investment.

The Undocu-Falcons Center, while providing
important resources, does not have the focused
mission or demonstrated outcomes of the Puente
Program, which is specifically designed to serve
Mexican American and Latina/o/x students. FLC
does not currently have a Puente Program and as
a Hispanic Serving Institution (HSI), this
represents a significant equity gap. Puente has a
proven track record across California of improving
transfer rates, retention, and academic success
through culturally relevant curriculum,
mentoring, and counseling. It is evident in our
student data that the absence of Puente is
impacting Latina/o/x student outcomes.
Establishing Puente at FLC is not only appropriate
but necessary to close achievement gaps and
fulfill our HSI mission.




                                                145
                                                                                           HOME



Hispanic / Latino: Action Plan   To support Latina/o/x students, FLC must address
(5000 characters max)            structural barriers, invest in long-term culturally
                                 grounded programming, increase Latina/o/x
                                 representation across administration, faculty and
                                 staff, and commit to creating a campus climate
                                 that affirms and empowers Latina/o/x student
                                 identities.

                                 Committed to advancing educational equity for
                                 undocumented students, students from
                                 mixed-status families, and Latina/o/x
                                 communities, FLC's action plan outlines current
                                 support systems and key strategies to improve
                                 access, retention, and success for these
                                 disproportionately impacted populations.
                                 Through intentional programming, partnerships,
                                 and culturally responsive practices, FLC aims to
                                 close equity gaps and fulfill its responsibilities as a
                                 Hispanic Serving Institution (HSI).

                                 During the Spring 2025 term, the FLC Academic
                                 Senate unanimously passed a resolution in
                                 support of the PUENTE Program at Folsom Lake
                                 College. FLC’s Diversity and Equity Committee
                                 unanimously voted in support of the same
                                 resolution.

                                 PUENTE is a proven, equity-focused model
                                 designed to increase college access, retention,
                                 and transfer for Chicana/o/x and Latina/o/x
                                 students. It combines counseling, writing
                                 instruction, and mentoring in a culturally
                                 affirming learning community. At FLC, PUENTE
                                 will work in collaboration with the Chicana/o/x
                                 Studies Program in the Ethnic Studies Department
                                 to provide students with culturally grounded
                                 instruction and wraparound support. This
                                 partnership will help address barriers such as:
                                 lack of culturally relevant curriculum and
                                 instruction, first-generation college navigation
                                 challenges, transfer preparedness, sense of
                                 belonging and community engagement. As an
                                 HSI, FLC has both the opportunity and obligation
                                 to implement Puente to better serve its growing
                                 Latina/o/x student population.

                                 Additionally, as mentioned in the BLACK/AFRICAN
                                 AMERICAN Intensive Focus Population, reinstating



                                                                                   146
                                                       HOME



a comprehensive equity training and mandating
this for new faculty as well as incentivizing it for
other faculty is a key strategy to better serve the
needs of our DI Population Students. These
trainings align with best practices as outlined by
the CCCCO and represent a means of ensuring
that faculty/staff are aware of special challenges
facing students and effective strategies and
practices for meeting these needs.

*Current Supports for Undocumented Students
and Mixed-Status Families
FLC’s Undocu-Falcons Center is a critical hub for
supporting undocumented students, students
from mixed-status families, and their allies. The
center is committed to student empowerment
and educational success, offering assistance with
application and enrollment processes; support
completing residency forms and the California
Dream Act Application (CADAA); financial aid
guidance and scholarship information;
connections to on-campus support programs such
as EOPS, DSPS, and Counseling; and referrals to
trusted off-campus community organizations.

 - To strengthen legal support services, the Los
Rios Community College District has partnered
with CHIRLA Legal Services to provide free virtual
and in-person immigration consultations to
students, staff, and faculty. These consultations
offer critical help with DACA renewals,
adjustment of status, and family-based
immigration matters.

Puente is a proven, equity-focused model
designed to increase college access, retention,
and transfer for Chicana/o/x and Latina/o/x
students. It combines counseling, writing
instruction, and mentoring in a culturally
affirming learning community. At FLC, Puente will
work in collaboration with the Chicana/o/x
Studies Program in the Ethnic Studies Department
to provide students with culturally grounded
instruction and wraparound support. This
partnership will help address barriers such as:
lack of culturally relevant curriculum and
instruction, first-generation college navigation
challenges, transfer preparedness, sense of



                                                 147
                                                       HOME



belonging and community engagement. As an
HSI, FLC has both the opportunity and obligation
to implement Puente to better serve its growing
Latina/o/x student population.

*Strategic Actions and Next Steps
To build on existing supports and fully implement
Puente, FLC will take the following steps:

 Program Development and Institutionalization
 - Secure funding and staffing for Puente,
including a designated Puente counselor, English
faculty, and program coordinator.
 - Identify and designate classroom and
community spaces for Puente activities and
cohort meetings.
 - Align Puente outcomes with FLC’s Student
Equity and Achievement (SEA) Plan and HSI
strategic goals.

Cross-Departmental Collaboration
 - Strengthen partnerships between the
Undocu-Falcons Center, EOPS, Counseling, and
Financial Aid to ensure holistic support for
undocumented and Latina/o/x students.
 - Build clear referral systems and communication
pathways between Puente and other student
services.

Culturally Responsive Faculty and Staff
Development
 - Provide training for Puente faculty and allies on
culturally responsive pedagogy,
immigration-related trauma, and strengths-based
support models.
 - Integrate equity and cultural humility modules
into ongoing professional development.

Community and Family Engagement
 - Host family information nights in Spanish and
English to support understanding of financial aid,
CADAA, residency, and transfer pathways.
 - Collaborate with community partners and local
high schools with high Latina/o/x and
undocumented student enrollment to recruit and
support future Puente participants.

Evaluation and Continuous Improvement



                                                148
                                                         HOME



 - Collect and analyze disaggregated data on
Latina/o/x and undocumented student success
metrics (enrollment, persistence, completion,
transfer).
 - Use feedback from Puente students and
Undocu-Falcons participants to inform future
programming and resource allocation.

*Long-Term Vision and Commitment
By establishing Puente and strengthening the
Undocu-Falcons Center, FLC affirms its
commitment to social justice, educational equity,
and student success. These efforts support not
only individual students but also their families
and communities, and reflect the values of equity
and inclusion at the heart of our Colleges Vision
and Mission.

* To ensure long-term impact, FLC will:
 - Prioritize sustained funding and institutional
support for equity programs
 - Incorporate Latina/o/x student voices in
planning and decision-making
 - Advocate at the district and state level for
expanded support for undocumented and
mixed-status students

Through these collective actions, FLC will fulfill its
role as an HSI and create a campus where
Latina/o/x and undocumented students are not
only welcomed—but empowered to thrive.




                                                  149
                                                                                               HOME



Native American: Challenges / Barriers   American Indian/Native American students at
(2500 characters max)                    Folsom Lake College face a distinct set of
                                         challenges rooted in historic trauma and
                                         marginalization, systemic inequities, and a lack of
                                         culturally responsive support. These barriers
                                         impact enrollment, persistence, academic
                                         success, and transfer outcomes.

                                         Access to stable, affordable housing remains a
                                         major obstacle. Students who travel from rural
                                         tribal communities or reservations to attend FLC
                                         are particularly vulnerable to displacement and
                                         housing insecurity, which interferes with their
                                         ability to focus on academic goals.

                                         FLC serves a geographically dispersed student
                                         population, including those from tribal lands and
                                         outlying rural areas. Limited public transportation
                                         options, long commute times, the increased cost
                                         of fuel and unreliable transportation create
                                         significant barriers to attending in-person classes
                                         or accessing campus resources.

                                         Native students at FLC report experiencing food
                                         insecurity. Without consistent access to nutritious
                                         meals, students may struggle with health, energy
                                         levels, and academic performance, compounding
                                         existing equity gaps.

                                         Student-parents, particularly in the Native
                                         community, often face challenges finding
                                         affordable and reliable childcare. This lack of
                                         support adds stress and limits participation in
                                         classes, student life, and support programs.
                                         Native students who are not from this area and
                                         do not have family close by, face challenges
                                         accessing Native community support.

                                         Native students at FLC face limited support when
                                         navigating the complex process of transferring to
                                         four-year institutions. The absence of clear,
                                         culturally informed advising pathways often
                                         leaves students without the guidance needed to
                                         meet transfer requirements confidently and
                                         successfully. In addition, students who finally
                                         become comfortable at FLC have a difficult time
                                         transferring without a 'warm hand off' and begin
                                         to experience imposter syndrome.



                                                                                        150
                                                                                      HOME




                               There is currently no consistent, long term
                               funding or infrastructure in place at FLC to
                               support Native cultural events or student
                               programming. A lack of cultural visibility
                               contributes to a sense of isolation and invisibility
                               for Native students on campus. Without spaces
                               that affirm their identity, many students feel
                               disconnected from the broader college
                               community.

Native American: Action Plan   To effectively support American Indian/Native
(5000 characters max)          American students, Folsom Lake College must
                               commit to intentional, long-term action that
                               addresses systemic inequities and fosters a
                               culturally affirming campus climate. This requires
                               investing in holistic, culturally rooted academic
                               support systems and securing permanent funding
                               for Native-led programs. Central to this
                               commitment is building authentic, sustained
                               partnerships with local tribes and Native
                               communities. These relationships ensure that
                               Native students are not only seen and heard, but
                               also valued, empowered in leadership and
                               decision-making roles, and fully supported
                               throughout their college journey.

                               FLC must prioritize long-term investment in
                               initiatives such as the Native American Student
                               Support & Success Program (NASSSP) and the
                               development of a Native Resource Center on the
                               main campus. Programs like NASSSP and the
                               development of a Native American Studies cohort
                               with the Native Studies Program and Ethnic
                               Studies Department offer key opportunities to
                               build targeted support structures rooted in
                               cultural knowledge and academic empowerment.
                               These efforts will create a strong, interconnected
                               network of academic, cultural, and social support,
                               essential to the success and well-being of Native
                               students.

                               In addition, FLC must increase American
                               Indian/Native American representation across
                               administration, faculty, and staff, and commit to
                               creating a campus climate that affirms and
                               celebrates tribal identity. This means actively
                               working with tribes whose homelands we occupy,



                                                                                151
                                                      HOME



integrating tribal traditional ecological knowledge
onto our campus reflecting value and respect of
the campus environment, cultural knowledge into
student services, and ensuring that American
Indian/Native American students feel a strong
sense of belonging on campus.

This action plan outlines specific strategies
organized by the student success framework:
Enrollment, Persistence, Math/English,
Completion, and Transfer.

*Successful Enrollment - Outreach and
Community Engagement:
Build and maintain relationships with local tribal
communities, tribal education offices, Indian
Education programs in K–12 schools, dual
enrollment, and local Native-led organizations.

*Cultural Presence:
Attend and support local Powwows, Big Times,
and other Native cultural events to build trust and
visibility. Sponsor and host these types of events
and academic conferences and institutes on
campus. Build a Native Resource Center
(inside/outside classroom) and Miwok Dance
Arbor open to tribes and local Native
communities. Restore the half acre wetland area
on campus to Native plants to be harvested by
local tribal communities and Native students for
classes.

*Enrollment Navigation:
Create accessible pathways for Native students to
apply, enroll, and access culturally supportive
services, including direct access to campus
support staff at cultural events and tribal spaces.

*Campus-Community Resource Linkages:
Offer wraparound enrollment support, such as
help with tribal enrollment paperwork, tuition
waivers, and financial aid application guidance.

*Persistence - Resource Awareness and Access:
Improve communication of existing support
services, and continually expand campus and
community partnerships that benefit Native
students.



                                                152
                                                        HOME




*Basic Needs Support:
Partner with Shingle Springs Tribal TANF, The
Sacramento Native American Health Center and
other tribal and county agencies to provide
housing and food security information and
resources.

*Expand emergency fund awareness and
availability through financial aid and scholarship
programs.

*Connect students to affordable childcare options
and transportation resources (bus, light rail, etc.).

*Equitable Access:
Provide priority registration guidance and
financial aid and scholarship workshops tailored
to Native students.

*Math & English Success - Cohort-Based Learning:
Establish cohorts through NASSSP, allowing
students to take foundational Math and English
courses together with a Native American Studies
transferable course to foster community,
academic confidence, and cultural resilience.

*Culturally Grounded Instruction:
Advocate for professional development on
culturally responsive pedagogy among Math and
English faculty to ensure inclusive, relevant
instruction.

*Completion - Major & Pathway Clarity:
Early and frequent engagement with academic
counselors and success coaches to guide major
selection and academic planning. Ensure that
counselors are trained in culturally responsive
practices and understand Native students’ unique
needs and pathways.

*Academic Support:
Strengthen connections between Native students
and tutoring and other student support
programs. Leverage NASSSP and other success
programs to ensure students are receiving course
completion resources, academic encouragement
and peer mentoring.



                                                153
                                                        HOME




*Transfer - Curricular Alignment:
Align FLC’s Native American Studies curriculum
with CSU and UC Native Studies programs to
ensure seamless transfer.
Collaborate with Sacramento State’s Native
American College program to provide warm
hand-off support for transfer students.

*Comprehensive Counseling:
Ensure students are meeting all graduation and
transfer requirements through proactive
counseling support. Host targeted transfer
workshops, campus tours, and advising sessions
for Native students.

*Internship & Career Support:
Partner with tribal organizations, nonprofit
agencies, and public institutions to increase
internship and career exposure opportunities.

*Institutional Commitment
Every part of the institution—including student
services, faculty, administration, financial aid, and
community outreach—must align to build a
support ecosystem for Native students. All staff
involved with Native student support should be
trained in Native cultural competency, historical
trauma, tribal sovereignty, and community
engagement. FLC must also prioritize recognition
and support for those leading this work, ensuring
equity work is shared, resourced, and valued.

By investing in these interconnected areas and
centering the voices and needs of Native
students, FLC can foster a culturally affirming
learning environment where Native
American/American Indian students feel seen,
supported, and empowered to achieve their
academic and personal goals.

Financial Aid. In coordination with your
Financial Aid Department, please summarize the      The Folsom Lake College Financial Aid
college's holistic plan to maximize financial aid   Department is committed to maximizing
receipt and systematically increase FAFSA           financial aid access and systematically
completion, especially among the identified DI      increasing FAFSA and CADAA completion
student populations within this Student Equity      among disproportionately impacted (DI)
Plan. This can include federal, state, and other    student populations. Our efforts are closely
campus aid programs (e.g., Pell Grant, Cal Grant,   aligned with the College’s Student Equity and
emergency aid, etc.) (2500 char max)                Achievement goals, particularly in supporting
                                                    Black/African American, Hispanic/Latino, and
                                                    Native American students, as well as First
                                                    Generation, LGBTQ, Foster Youth, DSPS,
                                                    Veterans, Homeless, and low-income
                                                    students.

                                                    Expanded Access and In-Person Support
                                                     To remove barriers for students across our
                                                    service area, the Financial Aid Department
                                                    provides in-person staffing at both the El
                                                    Dorado Center and Rancho Cordova Center
                                                    during peak times of the term. Virtual
                                                    support is available through the aid year with
                                                    Zoom drop in, phone, and email services. The
                                                    various modalities allow students—many of
                                                    whom face transportation, work, and family
                                                    obligations—to receive personalized financial
                                                    aid advising, application assistance, and
                                                    document submission support closer to
                                                    where they live. By embedding services at the
                                                    Centers, we can strengthen access for rural
                                                    and underrepresented populations.

                                                    Strengthening Support for Satisfactory
                                                    Academic Progress Appeal Processes
                                                     Recognizing the disproportionate impact of
                                                    Satisfactory Academic Progress (SAP)
                                                    standards on DI populations, we have
                                                    expanded the pool of trained SAP appeal
                                                    reviewers. This ensures timely review and
                                                    compassionate consideration of appeals,
                                                    reducing disruption of aid for students facing
                                                    academic or personal challenges. Our staff
                                                    emphasize equity-minded support to help




                                                                                               160
                                                  HOME



students navigate requirements and regain
eligibility.

Student and Faculty Engagement through
FLEX and Outreach​
 The Financial Aid Department hosts Financial
Aid-focused FLEX workshops for faculty and
staff, building campus-wide awareness of aid
programs and equipping the larger FLC
community with tools to refer students
effectively. The Department also participates
in college and community outreach
events—including Spot Light Night and Cash
for College workshops—to increase early
FAFSA and CADAA completion, particularly
among first-generation and low-income
families.

Targeted Aid and Equity Strategies​
 In addition to administering federal and state
aid such as Pell Grants, Cal Grants, and the
California College Promise Grant, Financial
Aid connects students to emergency aid,
scholarships, and specialized support.
Providing information about additional
resource opportunities are particularly
impactful for DI groups, including Foster
Youth (NextUp), Veterans benefits, DSPS
accommodations, and resources for
housing-insecure students.

DSPS. In coordination with your DSPS program,        Currently, the Disabled Students Programs
please summarize how your college DSPS               and Services (DSPS) department does not
program will proactively support efforts to          receive funding through SEAP. However,
eliminate disproportionate impact and meet the
                                                     our student population is referenced in
developed goals for the student populations
identified in this Student Equity Plan. (2500 char   the FLC Equity statement, while DSPS
max)                                                 actively works towards the equitable
                                                     goals of these student populations.

                                                     The Americans with Disabilities Act (ADA)
                                                     guarantees individuals with disabilities
                                                     the same opportunities as everyone else.
                                                     DSPS serves as a critical vehicle to fulfill
                                                     this mandate by providing
                                                     accommodations, services, and support to
                                                     students with disabilities. These
                                                     disabilities often impact students’ ability
                                                     to fully engage in daily academic and
                                                     social activities. A significant portion of
                                                     the DSPS population intersects with
                                                     groups identified as underrepresented
                                                     and marginalized, including Black/African
                                                     American, Hispanic/Latino, and Native
                                                     American students. Many DSPS students
                                                     are also first-generation college students,
                                                     members of the LGBTQ+ community,
                                                     foster youth, economically disadvantaged,
                                                     or experiencing homelessness.

                                                     Despite being underfunded, understaffed,
                                                     and lacking a dedicated DSPS center and
                                                     test proctoring space, our department
                                                     continues to provide essential
                                                     accommodations to ensure that students
                                                     with disabilities have equitable access to
                                                     education. Our mission is to help students
                                                     not only participate but thrive and reach
                                                     their full potential, because education
                                                     should belong to everyone.

                                                EOPS exemplifies the intersectional
                                                identities of our student population.
                                                Many EOPS students navigate multiple,
                                                overlapping challenges: they may be
                                                students with disabilities, members of the
                                                LGBTQ+ community, former foster youth,

                                                insecurity—often in addition to
                                                experiencing systemic inequities related
                                                to race, ethnicity, gender, and
                                                socioeconomic status. These students
                                                require not only equitable access to
                                                education but also the comprehensive
                                                support necessary for their success.

                                                Areas of support EOPS provides to its
                                                supported students include book grants,
                                                consistent support / contact, and direct
                                                appointments with an EOPS specific
                                                counselor. Each EOPS student has an up
                                                to date comprehensive SEP.

                                                EOPS and its affiliated programs are
                                                committed to meeting the needs of

                                                with Title V regulations. Unfortunately,
                                                EOPS currently does not reach all of the
                                                students it could: despite 21% of students
                                                identifying as first-generation at FLC in fall

According to student’s self-identification as a
current or former foster youth, in Fall 2024,
2.4% of FLC students identified as foster
youth. Yet, only 11 students across the entire
college participated in the NextUp program.
Similarly, the MIS system (on which the data
for the student equity plans is based) only
captures these incredibly low ‘verified’




                                            165
                                                                                                    HOME



                                                 numbers, leaving many current and former
                                                 foster youth with measurable gaps to fall
                                                 through the cracks at Folsom Lake College.

                                                  This disconnect signals a clear need for
                                                 improved engagement and support
                                                 structures. By strategically braiding SEAP and
                                                 NextUp funds, FLC can establish a position
                                                 dedicated to providing consistent guidance
                                                 and support for all foster youth on campus.

                                                 It is important to recognize that all of our
                                                 students - certainly those in equity programs
                                                 - identify with one or more of the populations
                                                 highlighted by SEAP. Ensuring support for
                                                 foster youth is not just a compliance issue, it
                                                 is an equity imperative.

program will provide financial assistance,

PREP-

The in-facility program will focus on
increasing the integrity of data collection and
disaggregation while creating bridges to
ensure students are making progress toward
meeting Cal-GETC requirements. A key
strategy includes developing guided pathways
specifically tailored for students in facilities,
alongside exploring equitable service delivery
and support using the Learning Management
System (LMS), Canvas. Strengthening
relationships between FLC and facility
partners will also be prioritized to ensure
seamless collaboration. In addition, the
program will explore equitable technology
that aligns with the requirements of each
facility, further supporting student success
and access, as well as making support
services such as DSPS available to students in
facilities.

                                                    in-person courses. However, we have few
                                                    of these available.

enrollment students eventually attend FLC
following high school graduation. For non-CCAP

Additionally, the demographics of dually enrolled
students currently do not match either the
service area, nor the student population of FLC,
with historically underserved and first generation
students still under represented.

FLC needs to undertake more efforts to better
align their dual enrollment programs with the
goals of SEAP, ensuring that the benefits of
concurrent college enrollment during high school
are extended to Intensive Focus Populations
identified in the SEAP Plan. One potential strategy
for doing so is to increase recruitment/outreach
efforts to DI Population Students in the
Mountainside Middle College High School at FLC’s
El Dorado Center campus. This effort could
substantially increase the number of Intensive
Focus Population students earning college credits
in high school and potentially earning their
AA/transfer degree concurrently with their high
school diploma.

Equity Center & FLC Pride Nest build safe,
respectful, and inclusive communities on
campus. SEA funds should primarily support
programming and operations. The Equity
Center operates on temporary staff and does
not have a full time classified staff, director or
even a designated budget. This needs to be
established.

Falcon Cares addresses students’ basic needs,
including food insecurity, homelessness, and
transportation challenges—critical for DI
student persistence and completion.

MESA serves historically underserved
students in STEM, offering resources to
support competitive transfer and career
entry. New to FLC, MESA will actively collect
data.

NASSSP & the Native American Resource
Center coordinate with Native American
Studies to create Learning Communities.
Success rates for native students served by
NASSP have seen early growth from 23-24 to
24-25.

Prison and Reentry Education Program (PREP)
serves students in correctional facilities and
post-release, most of whom are DI students.
PREP enables sentence reduction and AA
degree completion.

Rising Scholars – see Justice-Impacted
Students above.

SOAR to Success supports first-year students,
including DI populations, in building a strong
foundation for persistence, completion, and
transfer.

Funding is essential for all programs and
centers to employ directors, coordinators,
peer mentors, and student staff who serve

STATUS:
The District Curriculum Coordinating Committee reviews college proposals for new, modified, or deleted
courses and programs; general education guidelines, graduation competencies, grading policies, course
placement into disciplines, and discusses and acts on other curriculum related issues. The attached
curriculum was approved by committee consensus on October 31, 2025.

The District Curriculum Coordinating Committee membership includes the following representatives:
American River College: Faculty - Aaron Bradford (Chair), Andrew Halseth, Megen Bevens; Admin -
Doug Herndon/Angela Milano; Cosumnes River College: Faculty – Karl Zoller (Chair), Jorge Baca, Todd
Drybread; Admin - Robert Montanez/Michael Lawlor; Folsom Lake College: Faculty - Andi Hicks (Chair),
Suha Al Juboori, Hugo Balmaceda Gonzalez; Admin – Greg McCormack/Carlos Lopez; Sacramento City
College: Faculty - Renee Medina (DCCC Chair), Kandace Knudson, Duane Leonard; Admin - Devoun
Stewart/Deb Saks; Articulation: Shannon Gilley; District Academic Senate: Paula Cardwell; DO Admin:
Jamey Nye/Frank Kobayashi.


RECOMMENDATION:
It is recommended that the Board of Trustees approve the curriculum proposals for American River,
Cosumnes River, and Folsom Lake Colleges.

                      Los Rios Community College District
                 Recommendation for Adoption by the Board of Trustees

                                                                                           Nov 17, 2025

COURSE PROPOSALS

New to District Courses


Cosumnes River College

   1. HIST 369 History of the Italian Renaissance (3 units)

      Prerequisite: None.

      Advisory: Eligibility for ENGL C1000.

      54 hours lecture

      This course presents an overview of the intellectual, social, political, economic and cultural
      history of Italy in the Renaissance period from the fourteenth century to the end of the sixteenth
      century. It examines the historical forces and processes that shaped the societies of Italy during
      this era, including the experiences and perceptions of marginalized groups, in areas such as civic
      life, education, social organization, artistic and literary cultures, geography, ethics and politics.

      Justification: This course provides a regional historical approach, which facilitates an in-depth
      understanding of the origins of modern beliefs, culture, and political structures that continue to
      influence contemporary society, and will enhance the regional scope of the History Program
      major.


Folsom Lake College

   1. DANCE 389 Dance in the Digital Age (2 units)

      Prerequisite: None.

      18 hours lecture, 54 hours laboratory

      This course is designed for students who want to utilize digital media to successfully secure
      opportunities as a dance professional for employment, higher education, or transfer. Students
      will learn best practices on how to create a screen dance for use as a marketing device from the
      perspective of a dance artist and a choreographer. Students will work on a range of skills starting
      with the conception of ideas; the filming of dance on a smartphone, including location, lighting,
      camera angles and filters; to editing of videos; and final presentation. At the completion of the
      course, the student will have created a film on a smartphone that can be utilized for a dance
      company or school audition as well as one that showcases themselves as a choreographer,
      promoting themselves and their product. They will have the tools necessary to network
      themselves, building relationships to enhance self-promotion. The student will also create a
                                                                                                  176
                                                                                                       HOME

   website on their smartphone or on a computer to showcase their film and other information,
   thereby increasing awareness of their work and their brand. Students will need access to a
   smartphone to film, record and edit reels and to create a website. Students may also access a
   computer to create and edit a website.

   Justification: As technology advances in the 21st century, dance can be developed specifically
   for the small screen. To remain relevant, dancers must invent themselves and promote their work
   through screen dance, enhancing their ability for self-promotion and workplace competency.

2. FITNS 471 Conditioning II (1 unit)

   Prerequisite: FITNS 470 with a grade of "C" or better

   27 - 54 hours laboratory

   This course provides students with opportunities to improve sports conditioning and skills
   through advanced level 1 sports performance training techniques. Students will be required to
   wear appropriate workout attire and shoes.

   Justification: This course is being developed to provide students with an advanced performance
   enhancement opportunity that is not available in our current fitness offerings.

3. MEDTEC 325 Molecular Methods in Medical Laboratory Technology (2 units)

   Prerequisite: BIOL 400 with a grade of "C" or better

   20 hours lecture, 48 hours laboratory

   Introduces the principles and application of molecular diagnostics in the clinical laboratory.
   Purchase of personal protective equipment, including safety glasses and disposable gloves, is
   required. Students are further advised to ensure all their immunizations are current as blood and
   body fluids are utilized. Immunizations include, but are not limited to: Hepatitis B, Measles-
   Mumps-Rubella (MMR), Varicella Zoster, Tetanus-Diphtheria-Pertussis (Tdap), and Influenza.

   Justification: Molecular technology is growing exponentially in the clinical laboratory.
   Although the Medical Laboratory Technology program has previously attempted to integrate
   molecular methods into its core courses, these efforts have been inconsistent and lacked depth.
   Establishing a dedicated course in molecular technology not only allows for a renewed focus and
   commitment to this rapidly advancing technology, but also frees up valuable lab time in other
   core courses to concentrate on discipline-specific skills.

4. RADTEC 340 Career Development and Advancement (2 units)

   Prerequisite: None.

   Enrollment Limitation: Students must be admitted to the Radiologic Technology Program and
   have met all requirements for entry including, but not limited to: submitting an application,
   passing all drug, background, and immunology screening requirements, and paying their portion
   of the college’s liability insurance policy.

   36 hours lecture

                                                                                             177
                                                                                                      HOME

   This course introduces students to advanced modalities in medical imaging. The students will
   learn about Computed Tomography, Magnetic Resonance Imaging, Vascular and Cardiac
   Interventional Radiology, Diagnostic Medical Sonography, Nuclear Medicine and Molecular
   Imaging, Bone Densitometry, and Radiation Oncology. Students will also develop resumes and
   interview skills.

   Justification: The Allied Health Department is collaborating with Sutter Health to develop a
   Radiologic Technology program for the greater Sacramento region. This course is a required
   component of that program and provides students with the background needed to be successful.

5. RADTEC 346 Introduction to Mammography (2.5 units)

   Prerequisite: None.

   Enrollment Limitation: Students must be admitted to the Radiologic Technology Program and
   have met all requirements for entry including, but not limited to: submitting an application,
   passing all drug, background, and immunology screening requirements, and paying their portion
   of the college’s liability insurance policy.

   45 hours lecture

   This course introduces students to the field of breast imaging. This course will cover the views
   required for 2D and 3D imaging of the breast for both screening and diagnostic imaging.
   Students will learn about computed radiography and digital image acquisition. This course will
   also cover the quality assurance and quality control required for mammography and common
   pathologies found in breast imaging.

   Justification: The Allied Health Department is collaborating with Sutter Health to develop a
   Radiologic Technology program for the greater Sacramento region. This course is a required
   component of that program and provides students with the background needed to be successful.

6. RADTEC 347 Introduction to Computed Tomography (2.5 units)

   Prerequisite: None.

   Enrollment Limitation: Students must be admitted to the Radiologic Technology Program and
   have met all requirements for entry including, but not limited to: submitting an application,
   passing all drug, background, and immunology screening requirements, and paying their portion
   of the college’s liability insurance policy.

   45 hours lecture

   This course provides an introduction to the fundamental physical principles of Computed
   Tomography (CT), emphasizing patient care, clinical operations, and quality control practices.
   Students will explore the evolution of CT technology—from first-generation scanners to the
   advanced systems used in contemporary imaging. The curriculum also covers essential body
   scanning protocols, preparing students for practical application in clinical settings.

   Justification: The Allied Health Department is collaborating with Sutter Health to develop a
   Radiologic Technology program for the greater Sacramento region. This course is a required
   component of that program and provides students with the background needed to be successful.

                                                                                            178
                                                                                                            HOME

   7. TAFILM 337 Audio Production (3 units)

      Prerequisite: None.

      36 hours lecture, 54 hours laboratory

      This course serves as an introduction to the theory and practice of audio production for live
      performance, film, television and digital recording applications. Students will learn the
      fundamentals of sound design and aesthetics, microphone use, and digital recording equipment.
      Students gain hands-on experience recording, editing, mixing and mastering audio. Upon
      completion, students will have basic knowledge of applied audio concepts, production workflow,
      equipment functions, and audio editing software. Students may be required to purchase
      headphones and USB microphones.

      Justification: This course will allow students to complete University transfer equivalent work in
      audio production for film and digital media applications, as well as music production, radio, and
      television. This course will also serve as an elective toward the A.A. degree in Theatre Arts:
      Film

   8. TAFILM 342 Digital Non-Linear Editing (3 units)

      Prerequisite: None.

      36 hours lecture, 54 hours laboratory

      This course will provide an overview of the theory and practice of nonlinear editing for video
      and film utilizing nonlinear digital editing work stations. Student will examine the technical and
      aesthetic requirements of editing through the use of professional film and video dailies. Projects
      will explore computer graphics, computer animation, audio/visual applications and digital video.
      Students will need to provide their own portable hard drive.

      Justification: This course will be part of a future A.A. degree for transfer in film. It will
      complement the other film courses offered by the department.

Removal of Courses


American River College

   1. HCD 160 Applied Life and Success Skills (2 units)
      Prerequisite: None.
      36 hours lecture
      This course covers the skills and tools necessary for daily independent living and success as a
      college student. It focuses specifically on disability management, life skills, personal goals, and
      community and campus resources. It would also be appropriate for those students interested in a
      career with disability services.

      Justification: This course has not been offered in years and there is no future plan to offer this
      course.



                                                                                                      179
                                                                                                       HOME

2   HCD 337 Exploring Health Careers (1 units)
    Prerequisite: None.
    18 hours lecture
    This course provides an opportunity to obtain information about a variety of health professions.
    This course explores the requirements to enter various health fields, including education,
    licensures, and volunteer experience. In addition, it offers opportunities to observe and gain
    knowledge in order to choose an appropriate health career goal.

    Justification: We have a course with the same name HCD 336, and have no plan to offer this
    course.

3   INDIS 1000 Learning Communities (0.5 - 12 units)

    Prerequisite: None.

    Justification: This course has not been offered in nearly 10 years, nor has it been updated and
    revised recently. The college no longer supports learning communities using this course.

4. TECCOM 300 Introduction to Technical/Professional Communication (3 units)

    Prerequisite: None.

    Advisory: BUS 100 or 310 with a grade of "C" or better

    54 hours lecture

    This course emphasizes principles of reader-centered writing for the workplace, focusing
    specifically on aspects of technical and professional communication. It covers the writing of
    documents used in businesses, academia, industry, and government. These documents may
    include memos, letters, brochures, instructions and procedures, proposals, grants, technical and
    informational reports, web sites, blogs, and product documentation. This course is formerly
    known as ENGWR 342.

    Justification: The course is being removed as it was only required for a degree or certificate
    program that has since been discontinued.

5. TECCOM 310 Writing Digital Content (1 unit)

    Prerequisite: BUS 100, BUS 310, ENGL C1000, or TECCOM 300 with a grade of "C" or better

    Advisory: ARTNM 352, CISA 305, and CISW 300 with grades of "C" or better

    18 hours lecture

    This course emphasizes the writing of digital content for the workplace and for technical and
    business disciplines. It covers audience analysis, online formats, research techniques, and
    concise, grammatical writing. This course is formerly known as ENGWR 344.

    Justification: The course is being removed as it was only required for a degree or certificate
    program that has since been discontinued.


                                                                                              180
                                                                                                            HOME

   6. TECCOM 330 Writing Technical Manuals (1 unit)

      Prerequisite: BUS 100, BUS 310, ENGL C1000, or TECCOM 300 with a grade of "C" or better

      Advisory: ARTNM 352 and CISA 305 with grades of "C" or better

      18 hours lecture

      This course is designed for professionals in all fields who need to design, create, and revise user
      and reference manuals in print or online formats. The course offers strategies for audience and
      task analysis, product learning, document design, drafting, procedure writing, and revision. Word
      processing, desktop publishing, and/or online tools are used to produce a print or online manual.
      This course is formerly known as ENGWR 352.

      Justification: The course is being removed as it was only required for a degree or certificate
      program that has since been discontinued.


Cosumnes River College

   1. ADAPT 332 Adapted Aquatics (1 unit)

      Prerequisite: None.

      Enrollment Limitation: A physician's statement verifying the disability, contraindications, and
      recommended activities.

      54 hours laboratory

      This physical education class is a personal water safety and fitness class adapted for individuals
      who are physically challenged. The class will be tailored for the individual's abilities and needs.
      Topics covered will include stroke mechanics, water safety, movement through water, and the
      use of water for resistive exercises.

      Justification: This course is no longer meeting the needs of our students.

   2. ADAPT 333 Adapted Shallow Water Aerobics (1 unit)

      Prerequisite: None.

      Advisory: Although swimming skills are not needed, it is suggested that students are comfortable
      in an aquatic environment.

      9 hours lecture, 27 hours laboratory

      This class is designed for individuals who are physically challenged and uses the resistance of
      the water for low impact weight bearing exercise. It will be tailored to each student's individual
      abilities. The class will include exercises in shallow water; working on cardiovascular endurance
      and fitness, muscular strength and endurance as well as increase overall flexibility. Students
      must have a doctor's recommendation on file. No swimming skills are needed or required as
      students will be in shallow water and use equipment to help with flotation.

                                                                                                 181
                                                                                                       HOME

   Justification: This course is no longer meeting the needs of our students.

3. ADAPT 334 Adapted Deep Water Aerobics (1 unit)

   Prerequisite: None.

   Advisory: Although swimming skills are not necessary, it is strongly suggested that students
   enrolled in this course are comfortable in an aquatic environment, especially in deep water.

   9 hours lecture, 27 hours laboratory

   This course is designed to allow students with disabilities or challenges, the opportunity to
   improve their overall fitness through a no-impact, non-weight bearing class in the water. In this
   class, cardiovascular exercises and endurance will be stressed. Additionally, due to the resistance
   of the water, students will improve their overall strength and flexibility.

   Justification: We haven't offered this course in a number of years as it no longer meets the
   needs of our students.

4. BIOL 300 The Foundations of Biology (3 units)

   Prerequisite: None.

   Advisory: Eligibility for ENGWR 300

   54 hours lecture

   This course is a survey of major topics in the biological sciences for the non-science major with
   an emphasis on human biology. Units covered include cell structure and chemistry, metabolism,
   Mendelian and molecular genetics, genetic engineering, anatomy and physiology of humans,
   evolution, and ecology. Students interested in a general elective biology course are strongly
   advised to take either BIOL 300, BIOL 307, BIOL 308, or BIOL 310 since some transfer
   institutions will provide credit for only one of the four courses. 300-level biology courses may
   not be accepted by your transfer institution if taken after a 400-level biology course.

   Justification: We are no longer offering this course. It has been replaced by BIOL 308:
   Contemporary Biology

5. BIOL 307 Biology of Organisms (4 units)

   Prerequisite: None.

   Advisory: Eligibility for ENGWR 300

   54 hours lecture, 54 hours laboratory

   This is a general biology course focusing on a survey of the plant and animal kingdoms with an
   emphasis on evolution and biodiversity. The course covers the general principles of biology
   including: methods of science, cell organization, genetics, evolution, ecology, biodiversity, and
   anatomy. These principles are explored in more depth through the examination of additional
   topics which may include: disease and epidemiology, physiological ecology, biotechnology,

                                                                                             182
                                                                                                         HOME

   population growth and regulation, ecosystem ecology, and conservation biology. The course is
   designed for non-science majors and is especially useful for liberal studies, elementary
   education, environmental studies, recreation, and similar majors. Students interested in a general
   elective biology course are strongly advised to take either BIOL 300, BIOL 307, BIOL 308, or
   BIOL 310 since some transfer institutions will provide credit for only one of the four courses.
   300-level biology courses may not be accepted by your transfer institution if taken after a 400-
   level biology course.

   Justification: We are no longer offering this course. It has been replaced by BIOL 308:
   Contemporary Biology (3 units) and BIOL 309: Contemporary Biology Lab (1 unit).

6. BIOL 310 General Biology (4 units)

   Prerequisite: None.

   Advisory: Eligibility for ENGWR 300

   54 hours lecture, 54 hours laboratory

   This is a survey of biological science with an emphasis on human biology. This course is
   intended for non-science majors. Topics covered include scientific inquiry, cell structure,
   transmission and molecular genetics, major organ systems, evolution, and ecology. Major
   biological principles are explored in each topic, but an emphasis is placed on human issues. The
   laboratory activities are designed to further investigate and illuminate each topic area. Students
   interested in a general elective biology course are strongly advised to take either BIOL 300,
   BIOL 307, BIOL 308, or BIOL 310 since some transfer institutions will provide credit for only
   one of the four courses. 300-level biology courses may not be accepted by your transfer
   institution if taken after a 400-level biology course.

   Justification: We are no longer offering this course. It has been replaced by BIOL 308:
   Contemporary Biology (3 units) and BIOL 309: Contemporary Biology Lab (1 unit).

7. FITNS 323 Core Conditioning II: Fitness Ball (1 unit)

   Prerequisite: None.

   54 hours laboratory

   The fitness ball is a valuable tool for developing core strength (middle of your body), flexibility
   and balance. Because of the fitness ball's inherent instability, the effect of traditional strength
   training exercises is intensified when performed in conjunction with the fitness ball. Students
   will learn to safely and effectively execute strengthening exercises for all the major muscle
   groups with and without hand weights, flexibility exercises, and balancing exercises. Specific
   topics and exercises may vary each semester depending on the needs of the students.

   Justification: This course is no longer meeting the needs of our students. Most of the course
   topics can be accomplished by taking FITNS 324, Pilates.




                                                                                              183
                                                                                                     HOME

8. FITNS 355 Workforce Fitness (1 unit)

   Prerequisite: None.

   54 hours laboratory

   This physical education course emphasizes concepts related to health, physical fitness and
   recreation as they relate to the industrial worker. Exercise programs will be designed to improve
   specific muscle groups impacted in the occupational setting.

   Justification: This course is no longer meeting the needs of our students. Most of the course
   topics can be accomplished by taking FITNS 381, Weight Training.

9. HEED 350 Personal Wellness (3 units)

   Prerequisite: None.

   54 hours lecture

   This course takes a personal approach to lifetime healthy habits. The students will relate their
   own lives to topics that include: fitness, weight management, nutrition, stress management, risky
   behaviors and prevention of diseases. Evaluation, plans for change and reinforcement will be
   applied through the use of journals, projects, written assignments, physical assessments and
   exams. This course is the same as KINES 301, and only one may be taken for credit.

   Justification: This is a repetitive course as it is the same course as KINES 301. Students will
   still have the option to take the course.

10. JOUR 330 Computer Familiarization (2 units)

   Prerequisite: None.

   Advisory: BUSTEC 302

   36 hours lecture

   This is an introductory course to provide general knowledge on how computers work, computer
   terminology and the impact of computers on society and the work environment. Beginning level
   hands-on instruction using an operating system, word processing software, spreadsheet software,
   database software, email and the Internet will be emphasized. Students will be reading and
   interpreting written and oral instructions of a technical nature. This course is the same as CISC
   302, and only one may be taken for credit. See "Cross-Listed Courses" in the catalog.

   Justification: With permission of CIS faculty, the curriculum chair initiated the removal of this
   course, which is cross-listed with CISC 302. After consultation with Journalism professor, CIS
   faculty have decided to remove the cross-listing from this class. Students in Journalism typically
   have enough basic computer skills to successfully navigate the requirements of the program and
   do not need these fundamentals. In CIS, the course is still a popular choice for students who have
   not had prior experience with computers and is valuable for reducing barriers.



                                                                                             184
                                                                                                       HOME

11. TMACT 302 Soccer - Outdoor (1 unit)

   Prerequisite: None.

   54 hours laboratory

   The purpose of this course is to provide the student with beginning level knowledge and skills
   associated with outdoor soccer. This course emphasizes defense, offense, passing, ball control,
   heading, and shooting. It covers the skills, strategy, and rules that govern the play of outdoor
   soccer.

   Justification: This course no longer meets the needs of our students. Indoor Soccer, TMACT
   300, 301 and 308 will cover similar topics.

12. TMACT 303 Outdoor Soccer II (1 unit)

   Prerequisite: None.

   Advisory: TMACT 302; Student should have completed Outdoor Soccer I, played 1 year of High
   School Varsity Soccer, completed 1 year of College Soccer, or satisfy the professor with a level
   of proficiency.

   54 hours laboratory

   The purpose of this course is to provide the student with an intermediate level player
   environment to challenge and improve the player's knowledge and skills associated with outdoor
   soccer. This course emphasizes an intermediate level of defending, attacking, and technical
   ability. It covers the skills, strategy, and rules that govern the play of outdoor soccer. This class
   is not for beginners.

   Justification: This course no longer meets the needs of our students. Indoor Soccer, TMACT
   300, 301 and 308 will cover similar topics.

13. TMACT 304 Outdoor Soccer III (1 unit)

   Prerequisite: None.

   Advisory: TMACT 303; Student should have completed Outdoor Soccer II, played 2 years of
   High School Varsity Soccer, played at the competitive level of youth soccer, completed 1 year of
   College Soccer, or satisfy the professor with a level of proficiency.

   54 hours laboratory

   The purpose of this course is to provide the student with an advanced level player environment to
   challenge the player's knowledge and skills associated with outdoor soccer. This course
   emphasizes an advanced level of defending, attacking, and technical ability. It covers the skills,
   strategy, and rules that govern the play of outdoor soccer. This class is not for beginners or
   intermediate players.

   Justification: This course no longer meets the needs of our students. Indoor Soccer, TMACT
   300, 301 and 308 will cover similar topics.

                                                                                               185
                                                                                                     HOME

PROGRAM PROPOSALS

New Programs


American River College

   1. Hardwood Floor Layer Apprenticeship

      This program concentrates on training hardwood floor layer apprentices to the specific levels
      required for the construction industry and has been approved by the State of California Division
      of Apprenticeship Standards. It includes safety, blueprint reading, commercial construction
      processes, building codes, estimation, and various hardwood floor layer topics.

      Justification: The Carpenter's Training Committee for Northern California (CTCNC) has
      updated and expanded their course requirements for their Hardwood Floor Layers
      Apprenticeship Program. This update to the course offerings fulfills our obligation as LEA to the
      CTCNC in aligning the curriculum with the standards submitted to the State of California
      Department of Apprenticeship Standards (DAS). The expansion of this program increases the
      number of courses taken and the number of units students can complete, which allows us the
      opportunity to offer a new degree program in alignment with other CTCNC apprenticeship
      programs offered by the college.

   2. Hardwood Floor Layer Level I

      This program concentrates on training hardwood floor layer apprentices to the specific levels
      required for the construction industry and has been approved by the State of California Division
      of Apprenticeship Standards. It includes safety, blueprint reading, commercial construction
      processes, building codes, estimation, and various hardwood floor layer topics.

      Justification: The Carpenter's Training Committee for Northern California (CTCNC) has
      updated and expanded their course requirements for their Hardwood Floor Layers
      Apprenticeship Program. This update to the course offerings fulfills our obligation as LEA to the
      CTCNC in aligning the curriculum with the standards submitted to the State of California
      Department of Apprenticeship Standards (DAS). The expansion of this program increases the
      number of courses taken and the number of units students can complete, which allows us the
      opportunity to offer additional hardwood floor layer certificates in alignment with other CTCNC
      apprenticeship programs offered by the college.

Program Removals(s)


American River College

1.   Physics

        The Associate in Science in Physics for Transfer (AS-T) program provides students with a major
        that fulfills the general requirements for transfer to a California State University. Students with
        this degree will receive priority admission with junior status to the California State University
        system. Students should work closely with their American River College counselor to ensure that
        they are taking the appropriate coursework to prepare for majoring in Physics at the institution
        they wish to transfer to because major and general education requirements may vary for each
        CSU and the degree may only transfer to specific institutions. Students must complete the
        following Associate Degree for Transfer (ADT) requirements (Pursuant to SB1440, §66746): •
        60 semester or 90 quarter CSU-transferable units • the California General Education Transfer
        Curriculum (Cal-GETC) pattern • a minimum of 18 semester or 27 quarter units in the major or
        area of emphasis as determined by the community college district • obtain a minimum grade

                                                                                                    190
                                                                                                 HOME

point average (GPA) of 2.0 ADTs also require that students must earn a “C” or better in all
courses required for the major or area of emphasis. A "P" (Pass) grade is also an acceptable
grade for courses in the major if the course is taken on a Pass/No Pass basis. Although additional
preparatory courses are not required for this degree, students will be better prepared if they
complete differential equations, linear algebra, general chemistry, and at least one computer
programming course prior to transferring.

Justification: The state added more requirements to the template for this degree, pushing it well
above 60 units at our college. Since Physics is not an impacted major at any CSU campus,
students majoring in Physics do not need the guaranteed transfer benefit of an AS-T, so our
department decided to delete the degree rather than revise it to align with the new template.

   2. Technical Communications

       This certificate offers an interdisciplinary program of courses in Technical
       Communications, Art/New Media, and Computer Information Systems to prepare
       students for a variety of technical writing and professional communication careers. The
       certificate includes the theory, writing skills, design background, and computer
       applications knowledge needed for jobs in technical communication.

       Justification: This program has been discontinued due to low interest/enrollment. Any
       student enrolled in this program may replace the following: For Telecom 300 - WR 341
       @ CRC For Telecom 310 or 330 - CISC 362 (2) or CISW 321 (3) at ARC or SCC
       Petitions may be processed via BUS Dept AT ARC
